<div class="NavHead" style="font-size: 105%; background: transparent; text-align: left;">List</div>

<li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0">Ace, bank, bean, bread, bill, bone, buck, C-note, cheddar, dead president, deuce, dinero, dub, ducat, doubloon, fin, fiver, frog, grand, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Note" title="United States Note">greenback</a>, large, moolah, paper, <a href="/wiki/Sawbuck" title="Sawbuck">sawbuck</a>, scratch, scrilla, simoleons, single, skins, smackeroo, smackers, <a href="/wiki/Spondulix" title="Spondulix">spondulix</a>, stack, Tom, and yard<br/>
<b>Plural</b>: dough, green, bread, bones, bucks, skrilla, clams<br/>
<b>Based on denomination</b>: Washingtons, Jeffersons, Lincolns, Hamiltons, Jacksons, Grants, and Benjamins<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><br/>
<b>See also</b>: <a href="/wiki/Peso" title="Peso">peso</a> in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Piastre" title="Piastre">piastre</a> in Cajun <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a></li>
</ul>

<div class="NavHead" style="font-size: 105%; background: transparent; text-align: left;">3 non-U.S. territories</div>
<ul class="NavContent" style="list-style: none none; margin-left: 0; text-align: left; font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit;">

<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman"><img alt="Oman" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="600" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flag_of_Oman.svg/23px-Flag_of_Oman.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flag_of_Oman.svg/35px-Flag_of_Oman.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Flag_of_Oman.svg/46px-Flag_of_Oman.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Omani_rial" title="Omani rial">Omani rial</a> (higher value)<br/>
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama"><img alt="Panama" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="450" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Flag_of_Panama.svg/23px-Flag_of_Panama.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Flag_of_Panama.svg/35px-Flag_of_Panama.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Flag_of_Panama.svg/45px-Flag_of_Panama.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Panamanian_balboa" title="Panamanian balboa">Panamanian balboa</a> (at par)<br/>
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar"><img alt="Qatar" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="1400" height="9" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Flag_of_Qatar.svg/23px-Flag_of_Qatar.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Flag_of_Qatar.svg/35px-Flag_of_Qatar.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Flag_of_Qatar.svg/46px-Flag_of_Qatar.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Qatari_riyal" title="Qatari riyal">Qatari riyal</a><br/>
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia"><img alt="Saudi Arabia" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Flag_of_Saudi_Arabia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Saudi_Arabia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Flag_of_Saudi_Arabia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Saudi_Arabia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Flag_of_Saudi_Arabia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Saudi_Arabia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Saudi_riyal" title="Saudi riyal">Saudi riyal</a><br/>
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago"><img alt="Trinidad and Tobago" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="800" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg/23px-Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg/35px-Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg/46px-Flag_of_Trinidad_and_Tobago.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago_dollar" title="Trinidad and Tobago dollar">Trinidad and Tobago dollar</a><br/>
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates"><img alt="United Arab Emirates" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Flag_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates_dirham" title="United Arab Emirates dirham">United Arab Emirates dirham</a><br/>
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela"><img alt="Venezuela" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Flag_of_Venezuela.svg/23px-Flag_of_Venezuela.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Flag_of_Venezuela.svg/35px-Flag_of_Venezuela.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Flag_of_Venezuela.svg/45px-Flag_of_Venezuela.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Venezuelan_bol%C3%ADvar" title="Venezuelan bolívar">Venezuelan bolívar</a><br/>
<span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe"><img alt="Zimbabwe" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="840" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg/23px-Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg/35px-Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg/46px-Flag_of_Zimbabwe.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_bond_coins" title="Zimbabwean bond coins">Zimbabwean Bond Coins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwean_bond_notes" title="Zimbabwean bond notes">Zimbabwean Bond Notes</a> (at par)</li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>United States dollar</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Currency_sign" title="Currency sign">sign</a>: <b><a href="/wiki/Dollar_sign" title="Dollar sign">$</a></b>; <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">code</a>: <b>USD</b>; also abbreviated <b>US$</b> and referred to as the <b>dollar</b>, <b>U.S. dollar</b>, or <b>American dollar</b>) is the official <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">insular territories</a> per the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>. For most practical purposes, it is divided into 100 smaller <a href="/wiki/Cent_(currency)" title="Cent (currency)">cent</a> (¢) units, but officially it can be divided into 1000 <a href="/wiki/Mill_(currency)" title="Mill (currency)">mills</a> (₥). The circulating paper money consists of <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Note" title="Federal Reserve Note">Federal Reserve Notes</a> that are denominated in United States dollars (<a href="/wiki/Title_12_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 12 of the United States Code">12 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/12/418" rel="nofollow">§ 418</a>).</p>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Nixon_shock" title="Nixon shock">suspension in 1971</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[8]</a></sup> of convertibility of paper U.S. currency into any precious metal, the U.S. dollar is, <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Fiat_money" title="Fiat money">fiat money</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[9]</a></sup>. As it is the most used in <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international transactions</a>, the U.S. dollar is the world's primary <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">reserve currency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[10]</a></sup> Several countries <a href="/wiki/International_use_of_the_U.S._dollar" title="International use of the U.S. dollar">use it as their official currency</a>, and in many others it is the <a href="/wiki/De_facto_currency" title="De facto currency"><i>de facto</i> currency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[11]</a></sup> Besides the United States, it is also used as the sole currency in two <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories" title="British Overseas Territories">British Overseas Territories</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_British_Virgin_Islands#Dollarisation" title="Economy of the British Virgin Islands">British Virgin Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turks_and_Caicos_Islands" title="Turks and Caicos Islands">Turks and Caicos Islands</a>. A few countries use the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Note" title="Federal Reserve Note">Federal Reserve Notes</a> for paper money, while still minting their own coins, or also accept U.S. dollar coins (such as the <a href="/wiki/Susan_B._Anthony_dollar" title="Susan B. Anthony dollar">Susan B. Anthony dollar</a>). As of July 12, 2017, there was approximately $1.56 trillion in circulation, of which $1.52 trillion was in Federal Reserve notes (the remaining $40 billion is in the form of coins).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">Article I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Section_8:_Powers_of_Congress" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">Section 8</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">U.S. Constitution</a> provides that the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> has the power "To coin money".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[13]</a></sup> Laws implementing this power are currently codified at <a href="/wiki/Title_31_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 31 of the United States Code">31 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/31/5112" rel="nofollow">§ 5112</a>. Section 5112 prescribes the forms in which the United States dollars should be issued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Section5112_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Section5112-40">[14]</a></sup> These coins are both designated in Section 5112 as "legal tender" in payment of debts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Section5112_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Section5112-40">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sacagawea_dollar" title="Sacagawea dollar">Sacagawea dollar</a> is one example of the copper alloy dollar. The pure silver dollar is known as the <a href="/wiki/American_Silver_Eagle" title="American Silver Eagle">American Silver Eagle</a>. Section 5112 also provides for the minting and issuance of other coins, which have values ranging from one cent to 100 dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Section5112_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Section5112-40">[14]</a></sup> These other coins are more fully described in <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_United_States_dollar" title="Coins of the United States dollar">Coins of the United States dollar</a>.</p>
<p>The Constitution provides that "a regular Statement and Account of the Receipts and Expenditures of all public Money shall be published from time to time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[15]</a></sup> That provision of the Constitution is made specific by Section 331 of Title 31 of the United States Code.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[16]</a></sup> The sums of money reported in the "Statements" are currently being expressed in U.S. dollars (for example, see the <i>2009 Financial Report of the United States Government</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[17]</a></sup> The U.S. dollar may therefore be described as the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_account" title="Unit of account">unit of account</a> of the United States.</p>
<p>The word "dollar" is one of the words in the first paragraph of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denied_powers" title="Denied powers">Section 9</a> of Article I of the Constitution. There, "dollars" is a reference to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_milled_dollar" title="Spanish milled dollar">Spanish milled dollar</a>, a coin that had a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monetary" title="Monetary">monetary</a> value of 8 Spanish units of currency, or <a href="/wiki/Spanish_real" title="Spanish real">reales</a>. In 1792 the U.S. Congress passed a <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1792" title="Coinage Act of 1792">Coinage Act</a>. Section 9 of that act authorized the production of various coins, including "DOLLARS OR UNITS—each to be of the value of a Spanish milled dollar as the same is now current, and to contain three hundred and seventy-one <a href="/wiki/Grain_(unit)" title="Grain (unit)">grains</a> and four sixteenth parts of a grain of pure, or four hundred and sixteen grains of standard silver". Section 20 of the act provided, "That the money of account of the United States shall be expressed in dollars, or units... and that all accounts in the public offices and all proceedings in the courts of the United States shall be kept and had in conformity to this regulation". In other words, this act designated the United States dollar as the <i><a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">unit of currency</a></i> of the United States.</p>
<p>Unlike the Spanish milled dollar, the U.S. dollar is based upon a decimal system of values. In addition to the dollar the coinage act officially established monetary units of <i><a href="/wiki/Mill_(currency)" title="Mill (currency)">mill</a></i> or one-thousandth of a dollar (symbol <a href="/wiki/Mill_(currency)" title="Mill (currency)">₥</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Cent_(currency)" title="Cent (currency)">cent</a></i> or one-hundredth of a dollar (symbol ¢), <i><a href="/wiki/Dime_(United_States_coin)" title="Dime (United States coin)">dime</a></i> or one-tenth of a dollar, and <i><a href="/wiki/Eagle_(United_States_coin)" title="Eagle (United States coin)">eagle</a></i> or ten dollars, with prescribed weights and composition of gold, silver, or copper for each. It was proposed in the mid-1800s that one hundred dollars be known as a <i>union</i>, but no union coins were ever struck and only patterns for the $50 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_union" title="Half union">half union</a> exist. However, only cents are in everyday use as divisions of the dollar; "dime" is used solely as the name of the <a href="/wiki/Dime_(United_States_coin)" title="Dime (United States coin)">coin</a> with the value of 10¢, while "eagle" and "mill" are largely unknown to the general public, though mills are sometimes used in matters of tax levies, and gasoline prices are usually in the form of $X.XX9 per gallon, e.g., $3.599, more commonly written as $3.59<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>9</sup>⁄<sub>10</sub></span>. When currently issued in circulating form, denominations equal to or less than a dollar are emitted as <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_United_States_dollar" title="Coins of the United States dollar">U.S. coins</a> while denominations equal to or greater than a dollar are emitted as <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Note" title="Federal Reserve Note">Federal Reserve notes</a> (with the exception of gold, silver and platinum coins valued up to $100 as legal tender, but worth far more as bullion). Both one-dollar coins and notes are produced today, although the note form is significantly more common. In the past, "paper money" was occasionally issued in denominations less than a dollar (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fractional_currency" title="Fractional currency">fractional currency</a>) and gold coins were issued for <a href="/wiki/Circulation_(currency)" title="Circulation (currency)">circulation</a> up to the value of $20 (known as the "<a href="/wiki/Double_eagle" title="Double eagle">double eagle</a>", discontinued in the 1930s). The term <i><a href="/wiki/Eagle_(United_States_coin)" title="Eagle (United States coin)">eagle</a></i> was used in the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1792" title="Coinage Act of 1792">Coinage Act of 1792</a> for the denomination of ten dollars, and subsequently was used in naming gold coins. Paper currency less than one dollar in denomination, known as "fractional currency", was also sometimes pejoratively referred to as "shinplasters". In 1854, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Guthrie_(American_politician)" title="James Guthrie (American politician)">James Guthrie</a>, then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a>, proposed creating $100, $50 and $25 gold coins, which were referred to as a "Union", "Half Union", and "Quarter Union",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[18]</a></sup> thus implying a denomination of 1 Union = $100.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:One_US_dollar_1917.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="279" data-file-width="640" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/One_US_dollar_1917.jpg/200px-One_US_dollar_1917.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/One_US_dollar_1917.jpg/300px-One_US_dollar_1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/One_US_dollar_1917.jpg/400px-One_US_dollar_1917.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:One_US_dollar_1917.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Series of 1917 <a href="/wiki/United_States_one-dollar_bill" title="United States one-dollar bill">$1 United States bill</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Today, USD notes are made from cotton fiber paper, unlike most common paper, which is made of wood fiber. U.S. coins are produced by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint" title="United States Mint">United States Mint</a>. U.S. dollar <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">banknotes</a> are printed by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">Bureau of Engraving and Printing</a> and, since 1914, have been issued by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a>. The "<a href="/wiki/Large-sized_note" title="Large-sized note">large-sized notes</a>" issued before 1928 measured 7.42 by 3.125 inches (188.5 by 79.4 mm); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small-sized_note" title="Small-sized note">small-sized notes</a>, introduced that year, measure 6.14 by 2.61 by 0.0043 inches (155.96 by 66.29 by 0.11 mm). When the current, smaller sized U.S. currency was introduced it was referred to as Philippine-sized currency because the Philippines had previously adopted the same size for its legal currency.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dollar" title="Dollar">Dollar</a></div>
<p>In the 16th century, Count <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_Schlick" title="Hieronymus Schlick">Hieronymus Schlick</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> began minting coins known as <i>Joachimstalers</i> (from German <i>thal</i>, or nowadays usually <i>Tal</i>, "valley", cognate with "dale" in English), named for <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1chymov" title="Jáchymov">Joachimstal</a>, the valley where the silver was mined (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Joachim" title="St. Joachim">St. Joachim</a>'s Valley, now <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1chymov" title="Jáchymov">Jáchymov</a>; then part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a>, now part of the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeo_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo-45">[19]</a></sup> Joachimstaler was later shortened to the German <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taler" title="Taler">Taler</a></i>, a word that eventually found its way into Danish and Swedish as <i>daler</i>, Norwegian as <i>dalar</i> and <i>daler</i>, Dutch as <i>daler</i> or <i>daalder</i>, Ethiopian as ታላሪ (<i>talari</i>), Hungarian as <i>tallér</i>, Italian as <i>tallero</i>, and English as <i>dollar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatGeo_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-NatGeo-45">[19]</a></sup> Alternatively, <i>thaler</i> is said to come from the German coin <i><a href="/wiki/Guldengroschen" title="Guldengroschen">Guldengroschen</a></i> ("great guilder", being of silver but equal in value to a gold guilder), minted from the silver from Joachimsthal.</p>
<p>The coins minted at Joachimsthal soon lent their name to other coins of similar size and weight from other places. One such example, was a Dutch coin depicting a lion, hence its Dutch name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leeuwendaalder" title="Leeuwendaalder">leeuwendaler</a> (in English: <i>lion</i> daler).</p>
<p>The leeuwendaler was authorized to contain 427.16 grains of .75 fine silver and passed locally for between 36 and 42 <a href="/wiki/Stuiver" title="Stuiver">stuivers</a>. It was lighter than the large-denomination coins then in circulation, thus it was more advantageous for a Dutch merchant to pay a foreign debt in leeuwendalers and it became the coin of choice for foreign trade.</p>
<p>The leeuwendaler was popular in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a> and in the Dutch <a href="/wiki/New_Netherland" title="New Netherland">New Netherland Colony</a> (<a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>), and circulated throughout the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> during the 17th and early 18th centuries. It was also popular throughout Eastern Europe, where it led to the current Romanian and Moldovan currency being called <a href="/wiki/Romanian_leu" title="Romanian leu">leu</a> (literally "lion").</p>
<p>Among the English-speaking community, the coin came to be popularly known as lion dollar – and is the origin of the name <i>dollar</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[20]</a></sup> The modern American-English pronunciation of <i>dollar</i> is still remarkably close to the 17th-century Dutch pronunciation of <i>daler</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymologiebank.nl_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymologiebank.nl-47">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>By analogy with this lion dollar, Spanish pesos – with the same weight and shape as the lion dollar – came to be known as <i>Spanish</i> dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymologiebank.nl_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-etymologiebank.nl-47">[21]</a></sup> By the mid-18th century, the lion dollar had been replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dollar" title="Spanish dollar">Spanish dollar</a>, the famous "piece of eight", which was distributed widely in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish colonies in the New World</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nqteor_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-nqteor-48">[22]</a></sup> Eventually, dollar became the name of the first official American currency.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicknames">Nicknames</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Slang_terms_for_money#United_States" title="Slang terms for money">Slang terms for money § United States</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Colloquialism" title="Colloquialism">colloquialism</a> "<i>buck</i>"(s) (much like the British word "<i><a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">quid</a></i>"(s, pl) for the pound sterling) is often used to refer to dollars of various nations, including the U.S. dollar. This term, dating to the 18th century, may have originated with the colonial leather trade. It may also have originated from a <a href="/wiki/Poker" title="Poker">poker</a> term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[23]</a></sup> "<i><a href="/wiki/United_States_Note" title="United States Note">Greenback</a></i>" is another nickname originally applied specifically to the 19th century <a href="/wiki/Demand_Note" title="Demand Note">Demand Note</a> dollars created by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> to finance the costs of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_War" title="United States Civil War">Civil War</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">North</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[24]</a></sup> The original note was printed in black and green on the back side. It is still used to refer to the U.S. dollar (but not to the dollars of other countries). Other well-known names of the dollar as a whole in denominations include "<i>greenmail</i>", "<i>green</i>" and "<i>dead presidents</i>" (the last because deceased presidents are pictured on most bills).</p>
<p>A "<i><a href="/wiki/1000_(number)#In_popular_culture" title="1000 (number)">grand</a></i>", sometimes shortened to simply "<i>G</i>", is a common term for the amount of $1,000. The suffix "<i>K</i>" or "<i>k</i>" (from "<a href="/wiki/Kilo-" title="Kilo-">kilo-</a>") is also commonly used to denote this amount (such as "$10k" to mean $10,000). However, the $1,000 note is no longer in general use. A "<i>large</i>" or "<i>stack</i>", it is usually a reference to a multiple of $1,000 (such as "<i>fifty large</i>" meaning $50,000). The $100 note is nicknamed "<i>Benjamin</i>", "<i>Benji</i>", "<i>Ben</i>", or "<i>Franklin</i>" (after <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>), "<i>C-note</i>" (C being the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_numeral" title="Roman numeral">Roman numeral</a> for 100), "<i>Century note</i>" or "<i>bill</i>" (e.g. "<i>two bills</i>" being $200). The $50 note is occasionally called a "<i>yardstick</i>" or a "<i>grant</i>" (after President <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, pictured on the obverse). The $20 note is referred to as a "<i>double sawbuck</i>", "<i>Jackson</i>" (after <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>), or "double eagle". The $10 note is referred to as a "<i>sawbuck</i>", "<i>ten-spot</i>" or "<i>Hamilton</i>" (after <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>). The $5 note as "<i>Lincoln</i>", "<i>fin</i>", "<i>fiver</i>" or "<i>five-spot</i>". The infrequently-used $2 note is sometimes called "<i>deuce</i>", "<i>Tom</i>", or "<i>Jefferson</i>" (after <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>). The $1 note as a "<i>single</i>" or "<i>buck</i>". The dollar has also been referred to as a "<i>bone</i>" and "<i>bones</i>" in plural (e.g. "<i>twenty bones</i>" is equal to $20). The newer designs, with portraits displayed in the main body of the obverse (rather than in cameo insets), upon paper color-coded by denomination, are sometimes referred to as "<i>bigface</i>" notes or "<a href="/wiki/Monopoly_(game)" title="Monopoly (game)">Monopoly</a> money".</p>
<p>"<i><a href="/wiki/Piastre" title="Piastre">Piastre</a></i>" was the original French word for the U.S. dollar, used for example in the French text of the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>. Calling the dollar a piastre is still common among the speakers of <a href="/wiki/Cajun_French" title="Cajun French">Cajun French</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_England_French" title="New England French">New England French</a>. Modern French uses <i>dollar</i> for this unit of currency as well. The term is still used as slang for U.S. dollars in the <a href="/wiki/French_West_Indies#French_Caribbean" title="French West Indies">French-speaking Caribbean</a> islands, most notably <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dollar_sign">Dollar sign</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dollar_sign" title="Dollar sign">Dollar sign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Potosi_Real.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="409" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Potosi_Real.jpg/200px-Potosi_Real.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Potosi_Real.jpg/300px-Potosi_Real.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Potosi_Real.jpg/400px-Potosi_Real.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Potosi_Real.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Spanish_real" title="Spanish real">Spanish silver <i>real</i></a> or <a href="/wiki/Peso" title="Peso">peso</a> of 1768</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The symbol <b>$</b>, usually written before the numerical amount, is used for the U.S. dollar (as well as for many other currencies). The sign was the result of a late 18th-century evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Scribal_abbreviation" title="Scribal abbreviation">scribal abbreviation</a> "p<sup>s</sup>" for the <a href="/wiki/Peso" title="Peso">peso</a>, the common name for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_dollars" title="Spanish dollars">Spanish dollars</a> that were in wide circulation in the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> from the 16th to the 19th centuries. These Spanish pesos or dollars were minted in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_America" title="Spanish America">Spanish America</a>, namely in <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>; <a href="/wiki/Potos%C3%AD" title="Potosí">Potosí</a>, Bolivia; and <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a>, Peru. The <i>p</i> and the <i>s</i> eventually came to be written over each other giving rise to <i>$</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another popular explanation is that it is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Spain" title="Coat of arms of Spain">Spanish Coat of arms</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dollar" title="Spanish dollar">Spanish dollar</a>. These <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a> on the silver Spanish dollar coins take the form of two vertical bars (||) and a swinging cloth band in the shape of an "S".</p>
<p>Yet another explanation suggests that the dollar sign was formed from the capital letters U and S written or printed one on top of the other. This theory, popularized by novelist <a href="/wiki/Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand">Ayn Rand</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Atlas_Shrugged" title="Atlas Shrugged">Atlas Shrugged</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[29]</a></sup> does not consider the fact that the symbol was already in use before the formation of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_dollar" title="History of the United States dollar">History of the United States dollar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:500_USD_note;_series_of_1934;_obverse.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="1842" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg/200px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg/300px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg/400px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_obverse.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Obverse of rare 1934 $500 Federal Reserve Note, featuring a portrait of President <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a>.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:500_USD_note;_series_of_1934;_reverse.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="776" data-file-width="1843" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_reverse.jpg/200px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_reverse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_reverse.jpg/300px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_reverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_reverse.jpg/400px-500_USD_note%3B_series_of_1934%3B_reverse.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Reverse of a $500 Federal Reserve Note.</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The American <a href="/wiki/Dollar_coin_(United_States)" title="Dollar coin (United States)">dollar coin</a> was initially based on the value and look of the Spanish dollar, used widely in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_America" title="Spanish America">Spanish America</a> from the 16th to the 19th centuries. The first dollar coins issued by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint" title="United States Mint">United States Mint</a> (founded 1792) were similar in size and composition to the Spanish dollar, minted in Mexico and Peru. The Spanish, U.S. silver dollars, and later, Mexican silver pesos circulated side by side in the United States, and the Spanish dollar and Mexican peso remained legal tender until the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1857" title="Coinage Act of 1857">Coinage Act of 1857</a>. The coinage of various English colonies also circulated. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lion_dollar" title="Lion dollar">lion dollar</a> was popular in the Dutch New Netherland Colony (New York), but the lion dollar also circulated throughout the English colonies during the 17th century and early 18th century. Examples circulating in the colonies were usually worn so that the design was not fully distinguishable, thus they were sometimes referred to as "dog dollars".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. dollar was first defined by the Coinage Act of 1792, which specified a "dollar" to be based in the Spanish milled dollar and of 371 <a href="/wiki/Grain_(unit)" title="Grain (unit)">grains</a> and 4 sixteenths part of a grain of pure or 416 grains (27.0 g) of standard silver and an "eagle" to be 247 and 4 eighths of a grain or 270 grains (17 g) of gold (again depending on purity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[32]</a></sup> The choice of the value 371 grains arose from <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>'s decision to base the new American unit on the average weight of a selection of worn Spanish dollars. Hamilton got the treasury to weigh a sample of Spanish dollars and the average weight came out to be 371 grains. A new Spanish dollar was usually about 377 grains in weight, and so the new U.S. dollar was at a slight discount in relation to the Spanish dollar.</p>
<p>The same coinage act also set the value of an eagle at 10 dollars, and the dollar at <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>10</sub></span> eagle. It called for 90% silver alloy coins in denominations of 1, <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>, <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>, <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>10</sub></span>, and <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>20</sub></span> dollars; it called for 90% gold alloy coins in denominations of 1, <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>, <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span>, and <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>10</sub></span> eagles. The value of gold or silver contained in the dollar was then converted into relative value in the economy for the buying and selling of goods. This allowed the value of things to remain fairly constant over time, except for the influx and outflux of gold and silver in the nation's economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The early currency of the United States did not exhibit faces of presidents, as is the custom now;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[34]</a></sup> although today, by law, only the portrait of a deceased individual may appear on United States currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[35]</a></sup> In fact, the newly formed government was against having portraits of leaders on the currency, a practice compared to the policies of European monarchs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[36]</a></sup> The currency as we know it today did not get the faces they currently have until after the early 20th century; before that "heads" side of coinage used profile faces and striding, seated, and standing figures from Greek and Roman mythology and composite Native Americans. The last coins to be converted to profiles of historic Americans were the dime (1946) and the Dollar (1971).</p>
<p>For articles on the currencies of the colonies and states, see <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_pound" title="Connecticut pound">Connecticut pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delaware_pound" title="Delaware pound">Delaware pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_pound" title="Georgia pound">Georgia pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland_pound" title="Maryland pound">Maryland pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_pound" title="Massachusetts pound">Massachusetts pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_pound" title="New Hampshire pound">New Hampshire pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_pound" title="New Jersey pound">New Jersey pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_pound" title="New York pound">New York pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_pound" title="North Carolina pound">North Carolina pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_pound" title="Pennsylvania pound">Pennsylvania pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island_pound" title="Rhode Island pound">Rhode Island pound</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina_pound" title="South Carolina pound">South Carolina pound</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia_pound" title="Virginia pound">Virginia pound</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continental_currency">Continental currency</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="543" data-file-width="420" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg/200px-Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg/300px-Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg/400px-Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Continental_Currency_One-Third-Dollar_17-Feb-76_obv.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Continental One Third Dollar Bill (obverse)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_currency" title="Continental currency">Continental currency</a></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thirteen_colonies" title="Thirteen colonies">thirteen colonies</a> became <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">independent states</a>. Freed from British monetary regulations, they each issued <a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd" title="£sd">£sd</a> paper money to pay for military expenses. The <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> also began issuing "Continental Currency" denominated in Spanish dollars. The dollar was valued relative to the states' currencies at the following rates:</p>
<ul>
<li>5 shillings – <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a></li>
<li>6 shillings – <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">7<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> shillings – <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a></li>
<li>8 shillings – New York, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">32<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> shillings – <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Continental currency <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Depreciation_(currency)" title="Depreciation (currency)">depreciated</a> badly during the war, giving rise to the famous phrase "not worth a continental".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[37]</a></sup> A primary problem was that monetary policy was not coordinated between Congress and the states, which continued to issue bills of credit. Additionally, neither Congress nor the governments of the several states had the will or the means to retire the bills from circulation through taxation or the sale of bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[38]</a></sup> The currency was ultimately replaced by the silver dollar at the rate of 1 silver dollar to 1000 continental dollars.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silver_and_gold_standards">Silver and gold standards</span></h3>
<p>From 1792, when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mint_Act" title="Mint Act">Mint Act</a> was passed, the dollar was defined as 371.25 <a href="/wiki/Grain_(unit)" title="Grain (unit)">grains</a> (24.056 g) of silver. Many historians<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2009)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> erroneously assume gold was standardized at a fixed rate in parity with silver; however, there is no evidence of Congress making this law. This has to do with <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>'s suggestion to Congress of a fixed 15:1 ratio of silver to gold, respectively. The gold coins that were minted however, were not given any denomination whatsoever and traded for a market value relative to the Congressional standard of the silver dollar. 1834 saw a shift in the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> to 23.2 grains (1.50 g), followed by a slight adjustment to 23.22 grains (1.505 g) in 1837 (16:1 ratio).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1862, paper money was issued without the backing of precious metals, due to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>. Silver and gold coins continued to be issued and in 1878 the link between paper money and coins was reinstated. This disconnection from gold and silver backing also occurred during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>. The use of paper money not backed by precious metals had also occurred under the Articles of Confederation from 1777 to 1788. With no solid backing and being easily counterfeited, the continentals quickly lost their value, giving rise to the phrase "not worth a continental". This was a primary reason for the "No state shall... make any thing but gold and silver coin a tender in payment of debts" clause in <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Section_10:_Limits_on_the_States" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">article 1, section 10</a> of the United States Constitution.</p>
<p>In order to finance the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, Congress authorized the issuance of <a href="/wiki/Treasury_Note_(19th_century)" title="Treasury Note (19th century)">Treasury Notes</a>, interest-bearing short-term debt that could be used to pay public dues. While they were intended to serve as debt, they did function "to a limited extent" as money. Treasury Notes were again printed to help resolve the reduction in public revenues resulting from the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1857" title="Panic of 1857">Panic of 1857</a>, as well as to help finance the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>.</p>
<p>In addition to Treasury Notes, in 1861, Congress authorized the Treasury to borrow $50 million in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demand_Notes" title="Demand Notes">Demand Notes</a>, which did not bear interest but could be redeemed on demand for precious metals. However, by December 1861, the Union government's supply of specie was outstripped by demand for redemption and they were forced to suspend redemption temporarily. The following February, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Legal_Tender_Cases" title="Legal Tender Cases">Legal Tender Act of 1862</a>, issuing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Notes" title="United States Notes">United States Notes</a>, which were not redeemable on demand and bore no interest, but were <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">legal tender</a>, meaning that creditors had to accept them at face value for any payment except for public debts and import tariffs. However, silver and gold coins continued to be issued, resulting in the depreciation of the newly printed notes through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gresham%27s_Law" title="Gresham's Law">Gresham's Law</a>. In 1869, Supreme Court ruled in <a href="/wiki/Hepburn_v._Griswold" title="Hepburn v. Griswold">Hepburn v. Griswold</a> that Congress could not require creditors to accept United States Notes, but overturned that ruling the next year in the <a href="/wiki/Legal_Tender_Cases" title="Legal Tender Cases">Legal Tender Cases</a>. In 1875, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Specie_Payment_Resumption_Act" title="Specie Payment Resumption Act">Specie Payment Resumption Act</a>, requiring the Treasury to allow US Notes to be redeemed for gold after January 1, 1879. The Treasury ceased to issue United States Notes in 1971.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gold_Standard_Act" title="Gold Standard Act">Gold Standard Act</a> of 1900 abandoned the bimetallic standard and defined the dollar as 23.22 grains (1.505 g) of gold, equivalent to setting the price of 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troy_ounce" title="Troy ounce">troy ounce</a> of gold at $20.67. Silver coins continued to be issued for circulation until 1964, when all silver was removed from dimes and quarters, and the half dollar was reduced to 40% silver. Silver half dollars were last issued for circulation in 1970. Gold coins were confiscated by <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_6102" title="Executive Order 6102">Executive Order 6102</a> issued in 1933 by Franklin Roosevelt. The gold standard was changed to 13.71 grains (0.888 g), equivalent to setting the price of 1 troy ounce of gold at $35. This standard persisted until 1968.</p>
<p>Between 1968 and 1975, a variety of pegs to gold were put in place, eventually culminating in a sudden end, on August 15, 1971, to the convertibility of dollars to gold later dubbed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nixon_Shock" title="Nixon Shock">Nixon Shock</a>. The last peg was $42.22 per ounce<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> before the U.S. dollar was let to freely float on <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">currency markets</a>.</p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">Bureau of Engraving and Printing</a>, the largest note it ever printed was the $100,000 Gold Certificate, Series 1934. These notes were printed from December 18, 1934, through January 9, 1935, and were issued by the Treasurer of the United States to Federal Reserve Banks only against an equal amount of gold bullion held by the Treasury. These notes were used for transactions between Federal Reserve Banks and were not circulated among the general public.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coins">Coins</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_United_States_dollar" title="Coins of the United States dollar">Coins of the United States dollar</a></div>
<p>Official United States coins have been produced every year from 1792 to the present.</p>

<p>Discontinued coin denominations include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Half_cent_(United_States_coin)" title="Half cent (United States coin)">Half cent</a>: <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>¢, 1793–1857</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fugio_Cent" title="Fugio Cent">Fugio Cent</a>: 1¢, 1787</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two-cent_piece_(United_States_coin)" title="Two-cent piece (United States coin)">Two-cent piece</a>: 2¢, 1863–1873</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Three-cent_bronze" title="Three-cent bronze">Three-cent bronze</a>: 3¢, 1863 (not circulated)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Three-cent_nickel" title="Three-cent nickel">Three-cent nickel</a>: 3¢, 1865–1889</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Three-cent_silver" title="Three-cent silver">Trime</a>: 3¢, 1851–1873</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Half_dime" title="Half dime">Half dime</a>: 5¢, 1792–1873</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Twenty-cent_piece_(United_States_coin)" title="Twenty-cent piece (United States coin)">Twenty-cent piece</a>: 20¢, 1875–1878</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gold_dollar" title="Gold dollar">Gold dollar</a>: $1.00, 1849–1889</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Quarter_eagle" title="Quarter eagle">Quarter eagle</a>: $2.50, 1792–1929</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Three-dollar_piece" title="Three-dollar piece">Three-dollar piece</a>: $3.00, 1854–1889</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Eagle_(United_States_coin)" title="Eagle (United States coin)">Eagle</a>: $10.00, minted as a commemorative 1984–2003</li>

<p><b>Collector coins</b> for which everyday transactions are non-existent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[39]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Eagle_bullion_coins" title="American Eagle bullion coins">American Eagles</a> originally were not available from the Mint for individuals but had to be purchased from authorized dealers. In 2006 The Mint began direct sales to individuals of uncirculated bullion coins with a special finish, and bearing a "W" mintmark.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Silver_Eagle" title="American Silver Eagle">American Silver Eagle</a> $1 (1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troy_ounce" title="Troy ounce">troy ounce</a>) silver bullion coin 1986–present</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Gold_Eagle" title="American Gold Eagle">American Gold Eagle</a> $5 (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>10</sub></span> troy oz), $10 (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> troy oz), $25 (<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> troy oz), and $50 (1 troy oz) Gold bullion coin 1986–present</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/American_Platinum_Eagle" title="American Platinum Eagle">American Platinum Eagle</a> ($10, $25, $50, and $100 platinum coin) 1997–present</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_commemorative_coin" title="United States commemorative coin">United States commemorative coins</a>—special issue coins
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_union" title="Half union">$50.00 (Half Union)</a> 1915</li>
<li>Presidential Proofs (see below) 2007–present</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Technically, all these coins are still legal tender at face value, though some are far more valuable today for their <a href="/wiki/Numismatics" title="Numismatics">numismatic</a> value, and for gold and silver coins, their <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metal</a> value. From 1965 to 1970 the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_half_dollar" title="Kennedy half dollar">Kennedy half dollar</a> was the only <i>circulating</i> coin with any silver content, which was removed in 1971 and replaced with cupronickel. However, since 1992, the U.S. Mint has produced special Silver Proof Sets in addition to the regular yearly proof sets with silver dimes, quarters, and half dollars in place of the standard copper-nickel versions. In addition, an experimental <a href="/wiki/Stella_(United_States_coin)" title="Stella (United States coin)">$4.00 (Stella)</a> coin was also minted in 1879, but never placed into circulation, and is properly considered to be a pattern rather than an actual coin denomination.</p>
<p>The $50 coin mentioned was only produced in 1915 for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panama-Pacific_International_Exposition_(1915)" title="Panama-Pacific International Exposition (1915)">Panama-Pacific International Exposition (1915)</a> celebrating the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>. Only 1,128 were made, 645 of which were octagonal; this remains the only U.S. coin that was not round as well as the largest and heaviest U.S. coin ever produced.</p>
<p>A $100 gold coin was produced in High relief during 2015, although it was primarily produced for collectors, not for general circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1934 to present, the only denominations produced for circulation have been the familiar penny, nickel, dime, quarter, half dollar and dollar. The nickel is the only coin still in use today that is essentially unchanged (except in its design) from its original version. Every year since 1866, the nickel has been 75% copper and 25% nickel, except for 4 years during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> when nickel was needed for the war.</p>
<p>Due to the penny's low value, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Efforts_to_eliminate_the_penny_in_the_United_States" title="Efforts to eliminate the penny in the United States">efforts have been made to eliminate the penny</a> as circulating coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collector_coins">Collector coins</span></h3>
<p>The United States Mint produces Proof Sets specifically for collectors and speculators. <i>Silver Proofs</i> tend to be the standard designs but with the dime, quarter, and half dollar containing 90% silver. Starting in 1983 and ending in 1997, the Mint also produced proof sets containing the year's commemorative coins alongside the regular coins. Another type of proof set is the Presidential Dollar Proof Set where four special $1 coins are minted each year featuring a president. Because of budget constraints and increasing stockpiles of these relatively unpopular coins, the production of new Presidential dollar coins for circulation was suspended on December 13, 2011, by U.S. Treasury Secretary Timothy F. Geithner. Future minting of such coins will be made solely for collectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[43]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>2007 had George Washington, John Adams, Thomas Jefferson, and James Madison</li>
<li>2008 had James Monroe, John Quincy Adams, Andrew Jackson, and Martin Van Buren</li>
<li>2009 had William Henry Harrison, John Tyler, James K. Polk, and Zachary Taylor</li>
<li>2010 had Millard Fillmore, Franklin Pierce, James Buchanan, and Abraham Lincoln</li>
<li>2011 had Andrew Johnson, Ulysses S. Grant, Rutherford B. Hayes, and James A. Garfield</li>
<li>2012 had Chester Arthur, Grover Cleveland (1st term), Benjamin Harrison, and Grover Cleveland (2nd term)</li>
<li>2013 had William McKinley, Theodore Roosevelt, William Howard Taft and Woodrow Wilson</li>
<li>2014 had Warren G. Harding, Calvin Coolidge, Herbert Hoover, and Franklin D. Roosevelt</li>
<li>2015 had Harry S Truman, Dwight D. Eisenhower, John F. Kennedy, and Lyndon B. Johnson</li>
<li>2016 had Richard M. Nixon, Gerald R. Ford and Ronald Reagan.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dollar_coins">Dollar coins</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_United_States_dollar" title="Coins of the United States dollar">Coins of the United States dollar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%80%D1%81_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0_%D1%81_%D0%B8%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%BC_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BE%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D1%81_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%BC%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="291" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%80%D1%81_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0_%D1%81_%D0%B8%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%BC_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BE%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D1%81_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%BC%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg/200px-%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%80%D1%81_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0_%D1%81_%D0%B8%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%BC_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BE%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D1%81_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%BC%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%80%D1%81_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0_%D1%81_%D0%B8%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%BC_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BE%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D1%81_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%BC%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%90%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%80%D1%81_%D0%B4%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0_%D1%81_%D0%B8%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%B1%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B6%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%B5%D0%BC_%D0%A1%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%BE%D0%B4%D1%8B_%D1%81_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%81%D0%BF%D1%83%D1%89%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BD%D1%8B%D0%BC%D0%B8_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%D0%BC%D0%B8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowing_Hair_Dollar" title="Flowing Hair Dollar">Flowing Hair Dollar</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first United States dollar was minted in 1794. Known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowing_Hair_Dollar" title="Flowing Hair Dollar">Flowing Hair Dollar</a>, contained 416 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_(mass)" title="Grain (mass)">grains</a> of "standard silver" (89.25% silver and 10.75% copper), as specified by Section 13<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[44]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1792" title="Coinage Act of 1792">Coinage Act of 1792</a>. It was designated by Section 9 of that Act as having "the value of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_milled_dollar" title="Spanish milled dollar">Spanish milled dollar</a>".</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dollar_coin_(United_States)" title="Dollar coin (United States)">Dollar coins</a> have not been very popular in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[45]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_dollar_coin" title="United States dollar coin">Silver dollars</a> were minted intermittently from 1794 through 1935; a copper-nickel dollar of the same large size, featuring President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, was minted from 1971 through 1978. Gold dollars were also minted in the 19th century. The <a href="/wiki/Susan_B._Anthony" title="Susan B. Anthony">Susan B. Anthony</a> <a href="/wiki/Susan_B._Anthony_dollar" title="Susan B. Anthony dollar">dollar</a> coin was introduced in 1979; these proved to be unpopular because they were often mistaken for quarters, due to their nearly equal size, their milled edge, and their similar color. Minting of these dollars for circulation was suspended in 1980 (collectors' pieces were struck in 1981), but, as with all past U.S. coins, they remain <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">legal tender</a>. As the number of Anthony dollars held by the Federal Reserve and dispensed primarily to make change in postal and transit vending machines had been virtually exhausted, additional Anthony dollars were struck in 1999. In 2000, a new $1 coin, featuring <a href="/wiki/Sacagawea" title="Sacagawea">Sacagawea</a>, (the <a href="/wiki/Sacagawea_dollar" title="Sacagawea dollar">Sacagawea dollar</a>) was introduced, which corrected some of the problems of the Anthony dollar by having a smooth edge and a gold color, without requiring changes to vending machines that accept the Anthony dollar. However, this new coin has failed to achieve the popularity of the still-existing $1 bill and is rarely used in daily transactions. The failure to simultaneously withdraw the dollar bill and weak publicity efforts have been cited by coin proponents as primary reasons for the failure of the dollar coin to gain popular support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2007, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Mint" title="U.S. Mint">U.S. Mint</a>, under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidential_$1_Coin_Act_of_2005" title="Presidential $1 Coin Act of 2005">Presidential $1 Coin Act of 2005</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[47]</a></sup> introduced a new $1 U.S. Presidential dollar coin. Based on the success of the "<a href="/wiki/50_State_Quarters" title="50 State Quarters">50 State Quarters</a>" series, the new coin features a sequence of presidents in order of their inaugurations, starting with <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, on the obverse side. The reverse side features the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a>. To allow for larger, more detailed portraits, the traditional inscriptions of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E_Pluribus_Unum" title="E Pluribus Unum">E Pluribus Unum</a>", "<a href="/wiki/In_God_We_Trust" title="In God We Trust">In God We Trust</a>", the year of minting or issuance, and the mint mark will be inscribed on the edge of the coin instead of the face. This feature, similar to the edge inscriptions seen on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_pound_(British_decimal_coin)" title="One pound (British decimal coin)">British £1</a> coin, is not usually associated with U.S. coin designs. The inscription "Liberty" has been eliminated, with the Statue of Liberty serving as a sufficient replacement. In addition, due to the nature of U.S. coins, this will be the first time there will be circulating U.S. coins of different denominations with the same president featured on the obverse (heads) side (<a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Lincoln</a>/<a href="/wiki/Penny_(United_States_coin)" title="Penny (United States coin)">penny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Jefferson</a>/<a href="/wiki/Nickel_(United_States_coin)" title="Nickel (United States coin)">nickel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>/<a href="/wiki/Dime_(United_States_coin)" title="Dime (United States coin)">dime</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">Washington</a>/<a href="/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin)" title="Quarter (United States coin)">quarter</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">Kennedy</a>/<a href="/wiki/Half_dollar_(United_States_coin)" title="Half dollar (United States coin)">half dollar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Eisenhower</a>/<a href="/wiki/Dollar_coin_(United_States)" title="Dollar coin (United States)">dollar</a>). Another unusual fact about the new $1 coin is <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> will have two coins with two different portraits issued due to the fact he was the only U.S. President to be elected to two non-consecutive terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early releases of the Washington coin included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Error_coins" title="Error coins">error coins</a> shipped primarily from the Philadelphia mint to Florida and Tennessee banks. Highly sought after by collectors, and trading for as much as $850 each within a week of discovery, the error coins were identified by the absence of the edge impressions "E PLURIBUS UNUM IN GOD WE TRUST 2007 P". The mint of origin is generally accepted to be mostly Philadelphia, although identifying the source mint is impossible without opening a mint pack also containing marked units. Edge lettering is minted in both orientations with respect to "heads", some amateur collectors were initially duped into buying "upside down lettering error" coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[49]</a></sup> Some cynics also erroneously point out that the Federal Reserve makes more profit from dollar bills than dollar coins because they wear out in a few years, whereas coins are more permanent. The fallacy of this argument arises because new notes printed to replace worn out notes, which have been withdrawn from circulation, bring in no net revenue to the government to offset the costs of printing new notes and destroying the old ones. As most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vending_machines" title="Vending machines">vending machines</a> are incapable of making change in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banknotes" title="Banknotes">banknotes</a>, they commonly accept only $1 bills, though a few will give change in dollar coins.</p>

<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Obverse_of_the_series_2009_$100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg"><img alt="Obverse of the series 2009 $100 Federal Reserve Note.jpg" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="1200" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg/250px-Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg/375px-Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg/500px-Obverse_of_the_series_2009_%24100_Federal_Reserve_Note.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></td>

<p>The U.S. Constitution provides that Congress shall have the power to "borrow money on the credit of the United States".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[50]</a></sup> Congress has exercised that power by authorizing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Banks" title="Federal Reserve Banks">Federal Reserve Banks</a> to issue <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Notes" title="Federal Reserve Notes">Federal Reserve Notes</a>. Those notes are "obligations of the United States" and "shall be redeemed in lawful money on demand at the Treasury Department of the United States, in the city of Washington, District of Columbia, or at any Federal Reserve bank".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[51]</a></sup> Federal Reserve Notes are designated by law as "<a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">legal tender</a>" for the payment of debts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[52]</a></sup> Congress has also authorized the issuance of <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Note" title="Federal Reserve Note">more than 10 other types of banknotes</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Note" title="United States Note">United States Note</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[53]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Bank_Note" title="Federal Reserve Bank Note">Federal Reserve Bank Note</a>. The Federal Reserve Note is the only type that remains in circulation since the 1970s.</p>
<p>Currently printed denominations are <a href="/wiki/United_States_one-dollar_bill" title="United States one-dollar bill">$1</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_two-dollar_bill" title="United States two-dollar bill">$2</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_five-dollar_bill" title="United States five-dollar bill">$5</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_ten-dollar_bill" title="United States ten-dollar bill">$10</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_twenty-dollar_bill" title="United States twenty-dollar bill">$20</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_fifty-dollar_bill" title="United States fifty-dollar bill">$50</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_one_hundred-dollar_bill" title="United States one hundred-dollar bill">$100</a>. Notes above the $100 denomination stopped being printed in 1946 and were officially withdrawn from circulation in 1969. These notes were used primarily in inter-bank transactions or by <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">organized crime</a>; it was the latter usage that prompted President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> to issue an executive order in 1969 halting their use. With the advent of electronic banking, they became less necessary. Notes in denominations of $500, $1,000, $5,000, $10,000, and $100,000 were all produced at one time; see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large_denomination_bills_in_U.S._currency" title="Large denomination bills in U.S. currency">large denomination bills in U.S. currency</a> for details. With the exception of the $100,000 bill (which was only issued as a Series 1934 Gold Certificate and was never publicly circulated; thus it is illegal to own), these notes are now collectors' items and are worth more than their face value to collectors.</p>
<p>Though still predominantly green, post-2004 series incorporate other colors to better distinguish different denominations. As a result of a 2008 decision in an accessibility lawsuit filed by the <a href="/wiki/American_Council_of_the_Blind" title="American Council of the Blind">American Council of the Blind</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">Bureau of Engraving and Printing</a> is planning to implement a raised tactile feature in the next redesign of each note, except the $1 and the current version of the $100 bill. It also plans larger, higher-contrast numerals, more color differences, and distribution of currency readers to assist the visually impaired during the transition period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Means_of_issue">Means of issue</span></h2>
<p>The monetary base consists of coins and Federal Reserve Notes in circulation outside the Federal Reserve Banks and the U.S. Treasury, plus deposits held by depository institutions at Federal Reserve Banks. The adjusted monetary base has increased from approximately 400 billion dollars in 1994, to 800 billion in 2005, and over 3000 billion in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[55]</a></sup> The amount of cash in circulation is increased (or decreased) by the actions of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" title="Federal Reserve System">Federal Reserve System</a>. Eight times a year, the 12-person <a href="/wiki/Federal_Open_Market_Committee" title="Federal Open Market Committee">Federal Open Market Committee</a> meets to determine U.S. <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[56]</a></sup> Every business day, the Federal Reserve System engages in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_market_operations" title="Open market operations">Open market operations</a> to carry out that monetary policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[57]</a></sup> If the Federal Reserve desires to increase the money supply, it will buy securities (such as U.S. Treasury Bonds) anonymously from banks in exchange for dollars. Conversely, it will sell securities to the banks in exchange for dollars, to take dollars out of circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorkfed.org_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-newyorkfed.org-91">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the Federal Reserve makes a purchase, it credits the seller's reserve account (with the Federal Reserve). This money is not transferred from any existing funds—it is at this point that the Federal Reserve has created new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-powered_money" title="High-powered money">high-powered money</a>. Commercial banks can freely withdraw in cash any excess reserves from their reserve account at the Federal Reserve. To fulfill those requests, the Federal Reserve places an order for printed money from the U.S. Treasury Department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[59]</a></sup> The Treasury Department in turn sends these requests to the Bureau of Engraving and Printing (to print new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_notes" title="Federal Reserve notes">dollar bills</a>) and the Bureau of the Mint (to stamp the coins).</p>
<p>Usually, the short-term goal of open market operations is to achieve a specific short-term interest rate target. In other instances, monetary policy might instead entail the targeting of a specific exchange rate relative to some foreign currency or else relative to gold. For example, in the case of the United States the Federal Reserve targets the <a href="/wiki/Federal_funds_rate" title="Federal funds rate">federal funds rate</a>, the rate at which member banks lend to one another overnight. The other primary means of conducting monetary policy include: (i) <a href="/wiki/Discount_window" title="Discount window">Discount window</a> lending (as <a href="/wiki/Lender_of_last_resort" title="Lender of last resort">lender of last resort</a>); (ii) Fractional deposit lending (changes in the reserve requirement); (iii) Moral suasion (cajoling certain market players to achieve specified outcomes); (iv) "Open mouth operations" (talking monetary policy with the market).</p>
