<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:462px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="788" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg/460px-US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg/690px-US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg/920px-US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg.png 2x" width="460"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Consumer_Price_Index_Graph.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Consumer Price Index, starting from 1913</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 6th paragraph of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coinage_clause" title="Coinage clause">Section 8</a> of Article 1 of the U.S. Constitution provides that the U.S. Congress shall have the power to "coin money" and to "regulate the value" of domestic and foreign coins. Congress exercised those powers when it enacted the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1792" title="Coinage Act of 1792">Coinage Act of 1792</a>. That Act provided for the minting of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowing_Hair_Dollar" title="Flowing Hair Dollar">first U.S. dollar</a> and it declared that the U.S. dollar shall have "the value of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_milled_dollar" title="Spanish milled dollar">Spanish milled dollar</a> as the same is now current".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The table to the right shows the equivalent amount of goods that, in a particular year, could be purchased with $1. The table shows that from 1774 through 2012 the U.S. dollar has lost about 97.0% of its buying power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-measuring-worth_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-measuring-worth-94">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The decline in the value of the U.S. dollar corresponds to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Price_inflation" title="Price inflation">price inflation</a>, which is a rise in the general level of prices of goods and services in an economy over a period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[62]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Consumer_price_index" title="Consumer price index">consumer price index</a> (CPI) is a measure estimating the average price of consumer goods and services purchased by households. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Consumer_Price_Index" title="United States Consumer Price Index">United States Consumer Price Index</a>, published by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Labor_Statistics" title="Bureau of Labor Statistics">Bureau of Labor Statistics</a>, is a measure estimating the average price of consumer goods and services in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[63]</a></sup> It reflects inflation as experienced by consumers in their day-to-day living expenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[64]</a></sup> A graph showing the U.S. CPI relative to 1982–1984 and the annual year-over-year change in CPI is shown at right.</p>
<p>The value of the U.S. dollar declined significantly during wartime, especially during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, World War I, and World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[65]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a>, which was established in 1913, was designed to furnish an "elastic" currency subject to "substantial changes of quantity over short periods", which differed significantly from previous forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-powered_money" title="High-powered money">high-powered money</a> such as gold, national bank notes, and silver coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[66]</a></sup> Over the very long run, the prior gold standard kept prices stable—for instance, the price level and the value of the U.S. dollar in 1914 was not very different from the price level in the 1880s. The Federal Reserve initially succeeded in maintaining the value of the U.S. dollar and price stability, reversing the inflation caused by the First World War and stabilizing the value of the dollar during the 1920s, before presiding over a 30% deflation in U.S. prices in the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-central-banking-then-and-now_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-central-banking-then-and-now-100">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">Bretton Woods system</a> established after World War II, the value of gold was fixed to $35 per ounce, and the value of the U.S. dollar was thus anchored to the value of gold. Rising government spending in the 1960s, however, led to doubts about the ability of the United States to maintain this convertibility, gold stocks dwindled as banks and international investors began to convert dollars to gold, and as a result the value of the dollar began to decline. Facing an emerging <a href="/wiki/Currency_crisis" title="Currency crisis">currency crisis</a> and the imminent danger that the United States would no longer be able to redeem dollars for gold, gold convertibility was finally terminated in 1971 by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">President Nixon</a>, resulting in the "<a href="/wiki/Nixon_shock" title="Nixon shock">Nixon shock</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-historical-perspective_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-historical-perspective-101">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>The value of the U.S. dollar was therefore no longer anchored to gold, and it fell upon the Federal Reserve to maintain the value of the U.S. currency. The Federal Reserve, however, continued to increase the money supply, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Stagflation" title="Stagflation">stagflation</a> and a rapidly declining value of the U.S. dollar in the 1970s. This was largely due to the prevailing economic view at the time that inflation and real economic growth were linked (the <a href="/wiki/Phillips_curve" title="Phillips curve">Phillips curve</a>), and so inflation was regarded as relatively benign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-historical-perspective_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-historical-perspective-101">[68]</a></sup> Between 1965 and 1981, the U.S. dollar lost two thirds of its value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-measuring-worth_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-measuring-worth-94">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1979, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">President Carter</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Paul_Volcker" title="Paul Volcker">Paul Volcker</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Federal_Reserve" title="Chairman of the Federal Reserve">Chairman of the Federal Reserve</a>. The Federal Reserve tightened the money supply and inflation was substantially lower in the 1980s, and hence the value of the U.S. dollar stabilized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-historical-perspective_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-historical-perspective-101">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over the thirty-year period from 1981 to 2009, the U.S. dollar lost over half its value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-measuring-worth_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-measuring-worth-94">[61]</a></sup> This is because the Federal Reserve has targeted not zero inflation, but a low, stable rate of inflation—between 1987 and 1997, the rate of inflation was approximately 3.5%, and between 1997 and 2007 it was approximately 2%. The so-called "<a href="/wiki/Great_Moderation" title="Great Moderation">Great Moderation</a>" of economic conditions since the 1970s is credited to monetary policy targeting price stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-historical-perspective2_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-historical-perspective2-102">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is ongoing debate about whether central banks should target zero inflation (which would mean a constant value for the U.S. dollar over time) or low, stable inflation (which would mean a continuously but slowly declining value of the dollar over time, as is the case now). Although some economists are in favor of a zero inflation policy and therefore a constant value for the U.S. dollar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-central-banking-then-and-now_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-central-banking-then-and-now-100">[67]</a></sup> others contend that such a policy limits the ability of the central bank to control <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interest_rates" title="Interest rates">interest rates</a> and stimulate the economy when needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[70]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Nussbaum" title="Arthur Nussbaum">Nussbaum, Arthur</a> (1957). <i>A History of the Dollar</i>. New York: Columbia University Press. p. 56. <q>The dollar sign, $, is connected with the peso, contrary to popular belief, which considers it to be an abbreviation of 'U.S.' The two parallel lines represented one of the many abbreviations of 'P,' and the 'S' indicated the plural. The abbreviation '$.' was also used for the peso, and is still used in Argentina.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnited+States+dollar&amp;rft.aufirst=Arthur&amp;rft.aulast=Nussbaum&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+the+Dollar&amp;rft.date=1957&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=56&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Columbia+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
